"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Field_(geography)"" title=""Field (geography)"">field</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>spatially dependent variable</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A quantity that can be theoretically assigned to any point of space, such as temperature, soil moisture, or population density. Both scalar and vector fields are found in geographic applications, although the former is more common.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth"" title=""Figure of the Earth"">figure of the Earth</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The size and shape of the Earth as studied in <a href=""#geodesy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodesy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodesy</span></a>. Applications requiring varying levels of precision have led to the development of many different models of the Earth, ranging from simple spheres to much more accurate approximations such as <a href=""#geoid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geoid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geoids</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firn"" title=""Firn"">firn</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of ice that is at an intermediate stage between <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snow"" title=""Snow"">snow</a> and <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacial</span></a> ice. More specifically, firn is partially compacted <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N%C3%A9v%C3%A9"" title=""Névé"">névé</a> left over from past seasons which has subsequently recrystallized into a form that is harder and denser than névé.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firth"" title=""Firth"">firth</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Another name for a coastal <a href=""#inlet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § inlet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">inlet</span></a>, <a href=""#strait""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strait"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strait</span></a> or <a href=""#bay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bay</span></a> associated with the <a href=""#mouth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mouth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mouth</span></a> of a large <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a>, where the tidal effects of <a href=""#salt_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § salt water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">seawater</span></a> passing upriver have widened the riverbed into an <a href=""#estuary""><span title=""See entry on this page at § estuary"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">estuary</span></a>. The term is used primarily in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland"" title=""Scotland"">Scotland</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish_ladder"" title=""Fish ladder"">fish ladder</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A series of shallow steps down which water is allowed to flow, designed to permit <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salmon"" title=""Salmon"">salmon</a> to circumvent artificial barriers such as <a href=""#dam""><span title=""See entry on this page at § dam"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">dams</span></a> as they swim upstream to spawn.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-51"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fissure"" title=""Fissure"">fissure</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A long, narrow opening or line of breakage made by cracking or splitting, especially in rock or earth.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-52"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fjord"" title=""Fjord"">fjord</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also spelled <b>fiord</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A long, narrow marine <a href=""#inlet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § inlet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">inlet</span></a> with steep sides or <a href=""#cliff""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cliff"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cliffs</span></a> created by glacial erosion.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floodplain"" title=""Floodplain"">floodplain</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A broad, flat area of land adjacent to a <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a> or <a href=""#stream""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stream"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stream</span></a> which is leveled by annual flooding and by the lateral and downstream movement of <a href=""#meander""><span title=""See entry on this page at § meander"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">meanders</span></a>.</dd>
"
"focality"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The characteristic of a place that follows from its interconnections with more than one other place. When interaction within a region comes together at a single place (i.e., when the movement focuses on that location), the place is said to possess focality.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Focus_(earthquake)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Focus (earthquake)"">focus</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>hypocenter</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The point inside the Earth's crust from which an <a href=""#earthquake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § earthquake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">earthquake</span></a> originates.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foothills"" title=""Foothills"">foothills</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A geographic transition zone defined by gradual increases in elevation between plains or low-relief <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hills</span></a> and adjacent topographically higher hills, <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountains</span></a>, or <a href=""#upland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § upland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">uplands</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forest"" title=""Forest"">forest</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any extensive area dominated by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_(biology)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Community (biology)"">communities</a> of trees.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fresh_water"" title=""Fresh water"">fresh water</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any naturally occurring water characterized by low concentrations (typically less than 0.05% by volume) of dissolved salts and other solids relative to either <a href=""#salt_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § salt water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">salt water</span></a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brackish_water"" title=""Brackish water"">brackish water</a>. Sources of fresh water on Earth include <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glaciers</span></a>, <a href=""#ice_cap""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ice cap"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ice caps</span></a>, <a href=""#iceberg""><span title=""See entry on this page at § iceberg"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">icebergs</span></a>, <a href=""#bog""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bog"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bogs</span></a>, <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lakes</span></a>, <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">rivers</span></a>, <a href=""#stream""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stream"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">streams</span></a>, and most <a href=""#groundwater""><span title=""See entry on this page at § groundwater"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">groundwater</span></a>.</dd>
"
"functional diversity"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The characteristic of a place where a variety of different activities (economic, political, or social) occur, most often associated with <a href=""#urban""><span title=""See entry on this page at § urban"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">urban</span></a> places.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-53"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gazetteer"" title=""Gazetteer"">gazetteer</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A geographical dictionary or directory used in conjunction with a <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a> or <a href=""#atlas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atlas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atlas</span></a> and containing information concerning the geographical make-up, social statistics, and physical features of a <a href=""#country""><span title=""See entry on this page at § country"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">country</span></a>, <a href=""#region""><span title=""See entry on this page at § region"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">region</span></a>, or <a href=""#continent""><span title=""See entry on this page at § continent"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">continent</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesics_on_an_ellipsoid"" title=""Geodesics on an ellipsoid"">geodesic</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Geography-Terms-Flashcardseven better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_geography_terms> by making georgraphy terms contribution and <a hrf=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesy"" title=""Geodesy"">geodesy</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>geodetics</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The science of accurately measuring and understanding the Earth's <a href=""#figure_of_the_Earth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § figure of the Earth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geometric shape</span></a>, orientation in space, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_field"" title=""Gravitational field"">gravitational field</a> and how these properties change over time.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodetic_control_network"" title=""Geodetic control network"">geodetic control network</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>geodetic network</b>, <b>reference network</b>, or <b>control point network</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodetic_datum"" title=""Geodetic datum"">geodetic datum</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>geodetic system</b>, <b>geodetic reference datum</b>, or <b>geodetic reference system</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#geographic_coordinate_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geographic coordinate system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coordinate system</span></a> and set of reference points used for locating places on the Earth, which defines <a href=""#horizontal_datum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § horizontal datum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">horizontal</span></a> and <a href=""#vertical_datum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vertical datum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vertical</span></a> coordinates upon a particular <a href=""#reference_ellipsoid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reference ellipsoid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">reference ellipsoid</span></a> that approximates the <a href=""#figure_of_the_Earth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § figure of the Earth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">figure of the Earth</span></a>. Geodetic datums are widely used in <a href=""#geodesy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodesy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodesy</span></a>, <a href=""#navigation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § navigation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">navigation</span></a>, and <a href=""#surveying""><span title=""See entry on this page at § surveying"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">surveying</span></a> applications to translate positions indicated on paper or digital <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">maps</span></a> to their actual positions on the Earth; because the Earth is an imperfect ellipsoid, localized datums such as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ED50"" title=""ED50"">ED50</a> covering only specific countries or regions are often more accurate representations of their area of coverage than global standards such as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WGS_84"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""WGS 84"">WGS 84</a> of the <a href=""#world_Geodetic_System""><span title=""See entry on this page at § World Geodetic System"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">World Geodetic System</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodynamics"" title=""Geodynamics"">geodynamics</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A subfield of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysics"" title=""Geophysics"">geophysics</a> and <a href=""#earth_science""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Earth science"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Earth science</span></a> that studies the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamics_(physics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Dynamics (physics)"">physical dynamics</a> of the Earth by applying physics, chemistry, and mathematics to the understanding of how <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_convection"" title=""Mantle convection"">mantle convection</a> and other internal processes lead to <a href=""#plate_tectonics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § plate tectonics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">plate tectonics</span></a> and geological phenomena such as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_formation"" title=""Mountain formation"">mountain formation</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism"" title=""Volcanism"">volcanism</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthquake"" title=""Earthquake"">earthquakes</a>, and <a href=""#fault""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fault"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">faulting</span></a>, among others.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system"" title=""Geographic coordinate system"">geographic coordinate system</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinate_system"" title=""Coordinate system"">coordinate system</a> used in geography that enables every location on Earth to be specified by a set of numbers, letters, or symbols. Geographic coordinates are often chosen such that one of the numbers represents a vertical position such as <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> and two or three other numbers represent a horizontal position such as <a href=""#latitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § latitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">latitude</span></a> and <a href=""#longitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longitude</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographic_information_science"" title=""Geographic information science"">geographic information science</a> (GIS)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also abbreviated <b>GIScience</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The scientific study of data structures and computational techniques for capturing, representing, processing, and analyzing geographic information.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographic_information_system"" title=""Geographic information system"">geographic information system</a> (GIS)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any system of computer software tools designed to allow users to record, store, manipulate, analyze, manage, and present large sets of spatial or geographic data.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographic_Names_Information_System"" title=""Geographic Names Information System"">Geographic Names Information System</a> (GNIS)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A digital public-domain database developed by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Geological_Survey"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""U.S. Geological Survey"">U.S. Geological Survey</a> and the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Board_on_Geographic_Names"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""U.S. Board on Geographic Names"">U.S. Board on Geographic Names</a> which contains name and locative information about more than two million physical and cultural features located throughout the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States"" title=""United States"">United States</a> and its territories. Each feature recorded in the database receives a unique feature record identifier called a <i>GNIS identifier</i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geographical_mile"" title=""Geographical mile"">geographical mile</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A unit of length defined as the distance equal to one <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minute_of_arc"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Minute of arc"">minute of arc</a> along the Earth's <a href=""#equator""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Equator"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Equator</span></a>: approximately 1,855.3 metres (1.1528&#160;mi; 1.8553&#160;km). The precise length varies with the <a href=""#reference_ellipsoid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reference ellipsoid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">reference ellipsoid</span></a> used to approximate the <a href=""#figure_of_the_Earth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § figure of the Earth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">shape of the Earth</span></a>. Regardless of the particular ellipsoid, the length of one <a href=""#degree""><span title=""See entry on this page at § degree"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">degree</span></a> of <a href=""#longitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longitude</span></a> at the Equator is equal to exactly 60 geographical miles.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography"" title=""Geography"">geography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The scientific study of the lands, features, inhabitants, and phenomena of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth"" title=""Earth"">Earth</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoid"" title=""Geoid"">geoid</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The shape that the surface of the Earth's <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">oceans</span></a> would take under the influence of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth"" title=""Gravity of Earth"">Earth's gravity</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation"" title=""Earth&#39;s rotation"">rotational acceleration</a> alone, in the absence of other influences such as winds and <a href=""#tide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tides</span></a>. It is often characterized as the precise mathematical <a href=""#figure_of_the_Earth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § figure of the Earth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">figure of the Earth</span></a>: a smooth but irregular gravitational <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equipotential_surface"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Equipotential surface"">equipotential surface</a> at every point of which, by definition, the direction of the force of gravity is always perpendicular and <a href=""#spirit_level""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spirit level"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spirit levels</span></a> are always parallel. Its shape results from anomalies in the Earth's gravitational field caused by the uneven distribution of mass within and on the Earth's surface. A <a href=""#reference_ellipsoid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § reference ellipsoid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">reference ellipsoid</span></a> is an idealized approximation of the more complex and accurate geoid.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoinformatics"" title=""Geoinformatics"">geoinformatics</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The science and technology which develops and uses <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_science"" title=""Information science"">information science</a> infrastructures to address problems and analyze data within <a href=""#geography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geography</span></a>, <a href=""#cartography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cartography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cartography</span></a>, <a href=""#earth_science""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Earth science"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geoscience</span></a>, and related branches of science and engineering.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geolocation"" title=""Geolocation"">geolocation</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The identification or estimation of the real-world geographic <a href=""#location""><span title=""See entry on this page at § location"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">location</span></a> of an object, involving the generation of a set of <a href=""#geographic_coordinate_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geographic coordinate system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geographic coordinates</span></a> in order to determine a more meaningful description of location, such as a street address.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomatics"" title=""Geomatics"">geomatics</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>geospatial science</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The scientific discipline that involves gathering, storing, processing, and delivering geographic or <a href=""#spatial_reference_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spatial reference system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spatially referenced</span></a> information.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomorphology"" title=""Geomorphology"">geomorphology</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The study of the arrangement and form of the Earth's crust and of the relationship between these physical features and the geologic structures beneath.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-54"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_science"" title=""Earth science"">geoscience</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#earth_science""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Earth science"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Earth science</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geosphere"" title=""Geosphere"">geosphere</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The collective non-living parts of the Earth: the <a href=""#lithosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lithosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lithosphere</span></a>, the <a href=""#atmosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atmosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atmosphere</span></a>, the <a href=""#cryosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cryosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cryosphere</span></a>, and the <a href=""#hydrosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hydrosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hydrosphere</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-12"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geostatistics"" title=""Geostatistics"">geostatistics</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics"" title=""Statistics"">statistics</a> which involves the organization, management, and analysis of spatial and spatiotemporal datasets. Geostatistical algorithms are often incorporated in <a href=""#geographic_information_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geographic information system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">GIS</span></a> software applications.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghetto"" title=""Ghetto"">ghetto</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A section of a city occupied by members of a minority group who live there because of social restrictions on their residential choices. Originally, the term referred specifically to a section of a European city to which <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism"" title=""Judaism"">Jews</a> were confined.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-55"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_till"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Glacial till"">glacial till</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The mass of rocks and finely ground material carried by a <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacier</span></a> and deposited when the ice melts. This creates an unstratified material of varying composition.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-56"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glaciation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Glaciation"">glaciation</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;The process or state of being covered with a <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacier</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;Another name for a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_period"" title=""Glacial period"">glacial period</a>, an interval of time that is marked by colder temperatures and advancing glaciers.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-57"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacier"" title=""Glacier"">glacier</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A persistent mass of dense <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice"" title=""Ice"">ice</a> that is constantly moving under its own weight, and which is composed largely of compacted <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snow"" title=""Snow"">snow</a> that forms where the annual accumulation of snow exceeds its melting and sublimation over very long periods of time. Glaciers slowly deform and abrade the land beneath them, creating a huge variety of landforms including <a href=""#cirque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cirque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cirques</span></a>, <a href=""#moraine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § moraine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">moraines</span></a>, and <a href=""#fjord""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fjord"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">fjords</span></a>. They form exclusively on land and are distinct from the much thinner ice that forms on bodies of water.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-13"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glade_(geography)"" title=""Glade (geography)"">glade</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>clearing</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any large, open, mostly treeless area within a <a href=""#forest""><span title=""See entry on this page at § forest"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forest</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glen"" title=""Glen"">glen</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A long <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a> bounded by gently sloping, concave sides, and typically narrower and deeper than a <a href=""#strath""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strath"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strath</span></a>. The term is used primarily in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland"" title=""Scotland"">Scotland</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_city"" title=""Global city"">global city</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>world city</b>, <b>power city</b>, or <b>alpha city</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#city""><span title=""See entry on this page at § city"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">city</span></a> which functions as an important or primary node in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_economy"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Global economy"">global economy</a>. Though criteria are not strictly defined, a global city typically is very large; dominates trade and economic interactions within a large surrounding area; supports a large and demographically diverse population; serves as a center of ideas and innovation in business, science, culture, and politics; and/or is a headquarters for major financial institutions, multinational corporations, or worldwide media and communications networks.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Positioning_System"" title=""Global Positioning System"">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#satellite_navigation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § satellite navigation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">satellite</span></a>-based <a href=""#radionavigation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radionavigation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radionavigation</span></a> <a href=""#positioning_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § positioning system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">positioning system</span></a> owned and operated by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense"" title=""United States Department of Defense"">United States Department of Defense</a> and made available for use by both the military and the general public. It is one of several <a href=""#global_navigation_satellite_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § global navigation satellite system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">GNSS</span></a> standards that provides <a href=""#geolocation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geolocation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geolocation</span></a> and time information, transmitted via <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microwave"" title=""Microwave"">microwave</a> signals, to enabled <a href=""#satellite_navigation_device""><span title=""See entry on this page at § satellite navigation device"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">satellite navigation devices</span></a>, known as <i>GPS receivers</i>, anywhere on or near the Earth where there is an unobstructed line of sight to at least four GPS satellites. Modern state-of-the-art GPS receivers can accurately pinpoint locations to within 30 centimetres (0.98&#160;ft).</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globalization"" title=""Globalization"">globalization</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The process of interaction and integration among people, companies, governments, and cultures across the world. A complex and multifaceted phenomenon, globalization is considered largely the result of economically motivated advances in transportation and communication technologies in the past several centuries which have dramatically increased interactions between otherwise isolated groups of people.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Globe"" title=""Globe"">globe</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A true-to-scale <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a> of the Earth that duplicates its round shape and correctly represents relative areas, sizes, and shapes of physical features, distances, and directions.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-9"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gnamma"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gnamma"">gnamma</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#panhole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § panhole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">panhole</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorge"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gorge"">gorge</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#canyon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § canyon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">canyon</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graben"" title=""Graben"">graben</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#depression""><span title=""See entry on this page at § depression"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">depression</span></a> or <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a> bounded on either side by distinct, parallel <a href=""#escarpment""><span title=""See entry on this page at § escarpment"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">escarpments</span></a> or <a href=""#fault""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fault"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">faults</span></a> and formed by the downward displacement of a block of the Earth's <a href=""#crust""><span title=""See entry on this page at § crust"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">crust</span></a>. Grabens often occur side-by-side with <a href=""#horst""><span title=""See entry on this page at § horst"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">horsts</span></a>, their uplifted or non-displaced counterparts, in a repeated series of vertical displacements.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_circle"" title=""Great circle"">great circle</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called an <b>orthodrome</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any circle on the surface of a sphere created by the intersection of the sphere and a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_(geometry)"" title=""Plane (geometry)"">plane</a> that passes through its center. A great circle divides the sphere into two equal hemispheres, and all of a sphere's great circles have the same center and circumference as each other, which by definition is the largest possible circumference of the sphere. The mathematical properties of great circles make them useful in <a href=""#geodesy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodesy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodesy</span></a>, where they are often visualized upon the surface of the Earth (despite the fact that the Earth is <a href=""#figure_of_the_Earth""><span title=""See entry on this page at § figure of the Earth"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">not a perfect sphere</span></a>): for example, the <a href=""#equator""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Equator"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Equator</span></a> of the idealized Earth is a great circle, and any <a href=""#longitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">meridian</span></a> with its antimeridian forms a great circle. Because the shortest path between any two points on the surface of a sphere follows the arc of a great circle, <a href=""#great-circle_distance""><span title=""See entry on this page at § great-circle distance"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">great-circle distances</span></a> are often used as approximations of <a href=""#geodesic""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodesic"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodesics</span></a> for the purposes of air and sea navigation.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great-circle_distance"" title=""Great-circle distance"">great-circle distance</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>orthodromic distance</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A route which follows the arc of a <a href=""#great_circle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § great circle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">great circle</span></a> as defined by the intersection of the Earth's surface with an imaginary plane passing through the Earth's center. It is the shortest route between two places on the Earth's surface.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-58"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grid_reference"" title=""Grid reference"">grid</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A pattern of lines on a chart or <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a>, such as those representing <a href=""#latitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § latitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">latitude</span></a> and <a href=""#longitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longitude</span></a>, which helps determine <a href=""#absolute_location""><span title=""See entry on this page at § absolute location"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">absolute location</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-10"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grid_north"" title=""Grid north"">grid north</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The direction northwards as indicated by the <a href=""#grid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § grid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">grid lines</span></a> of a <a href=""#map_projection""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map projection"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map projection</span></a>, which may or may not be aligned with <a href=""#true_north""><span title=""See entry on this page at § true north"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodetic north</span></a>. Grid north may also differ from <a href=""#magnetic_north""><span title=""See entry on this page at § magnetic north"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">magnetic north</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groundwater"" title=""Groundwater"">groundwater</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The water present beneath the Earth's surface in soil pore spaces and in fractures and voids within geological <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratum"" title=""Stratum"">strata</a>. Contrast <i><a href=""#surface_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § surface water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">surface water</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grove_(nature)"" title=""Grove (nature)"">grove</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A small group of trees growing close together and generally surrounded by little or no undergrowth.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Growing_season"" title=""Growing season"">growing season</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The part of the year during which local weather conditions (i.e. temperature and precipitation) permit the normal growth of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant"" title=""Plant"">plants</a> in a given location. What defines a ""growing season"" is often informal and colloquial, and may vary widely by location and from year to year; in many places, the local growing season is defined as the period of time between the average date of the last <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frost"" title=""Frost"">frost</a> (in temperate parts of the <a href=""#northern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Northern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Northern Hemisphere</span></a>, this typically occurs in the spring) to the average date of the first frost (in the autumn).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-59"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Groyne"" title=""Groyne"">groyne</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A rigid, man-made hydraulic structure extending from an ocean <a href=""#shore""><span title=""See entry on this page at § shore"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">shore</span></a> or river <a href=""#bank""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bank"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bank</span></a>, constructed to interrupt water flow and limit the movement of sediment by <a href=""#longshore_drift""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longshore drift"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longshore drift</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulch"" title=""Gulch"">gulch</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A deep, V-shaped <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a> formed by erosion, often containing a small stream or a dry <a href=""#streambed""><span title=""See entry on this page at § streambed"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">streambed</span></a>, especially one in arid regions.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_(geography)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gulf (geography)"">gulf</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A large arm or <a href=""#inlet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § inlet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">inlet</span></a> of an <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a> or <a href=""#sea""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sea"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sea</span></a> that lies within a curved coastline, similar to a <a href=""#bay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bay</span></a> but usually larger and often with a narrower opening.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gully"" title=""Gully"">gully</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A landform resembling a large ditch or a small <a href=""#ravine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ravine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ravine</span></a> created by the action of swift running water eroding deeply and sharply into soil, typically on a hillside.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guyot"" title=""Guyot"">guyot</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>tablemount</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">An isolated underwater <a href=""#seamount""><span title=""See entry on this page at § seamount"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">seamount</span></a> with a flat top that is at least 200 metres (660&#160;ft) below the water's surface.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamlet_(place)"" title=""Hamlet (place)"">hamlet</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A small human settlement, variably defined as one the size of a <a href=""#town""><span title=""See entry on this page at § town"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">town</span></a>, <a href=""#village""><span title=""See entry on this page at § village"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">village</span></a>, or <a href=""#parish""><span title=""See entry on this page at § parish"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">parish</span></a> or as a smaller subdivision of or satellite entity to a larger settlement.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanging_valley"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hanging valley"">hanging valley</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#tributary""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tributary"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tributary</span></a> <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a> that is higher in elevation than the main valley into which it drains, such that it appears to be ""hanging"" above the lower valley. Hanging valleys are commonly the result of differential <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacial</span></a> erosion, when adjacent areas beneath a glacier are subjected to different rates of erosion.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_tremor"" title=""Harmonic tremor"">harmonic tremor</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">One of a series of continuous rhythmic <a href=""#earthquake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § earthquake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">earthquakes</span></a> in the Earth's upper <a href=""#lithosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lithosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lithosphere</span></a> that can be detected by <a href=""#seismograph""><span title=""See entry on this page at § seismograph"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">seismographs</span></a>. Harmonic tremors often precede or accompany <a href=""#volcano""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volcano"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volcanic eruptions</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-14"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heading_(navigation)"" title=""Heading (navigation)"">heading</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#compass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § compass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">compass</span></a> <a href=""#cardinal_directions""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cardinal directions"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">direction</span></a> in which the bow or nose of a moving vessel or aircraft is pointed. This is not necessarily the same direction in which the vessel is actually traveling, known as its <a href=""#course""><span title=""See entry on this page at § course"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">course</span></a>; any difference between heading and course is due to the motion of the medium (the air or water) through which the vessel is moving, or other aerodynamic effects such as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skid_(aerodynamics)"" title=""Skid (aerodynamics)"">skidding</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slip_(aerodynamics)"" title=""Slip (aerodynamics)"">slipping</a>. See also <i><a href=""#bearing""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bearing"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bearing</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Headland"" title=""Headland"">headland</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A high coastal <a href=""#promontory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § promontory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">promontory</span></a> that extends out into a body of water, often surrounded by steep <a href=""#cliff""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cliff"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cliffs</span></a>. A very large headland is often called a <a href=""#cape""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cape"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cape</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hearth"" title=""Hearth"">hearth</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The source area of any innovation. The source area from which an idea, crop, artifact, or good is diffused to other areas.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-60"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/heartland"" class=""extiw"" title=""wikt:heartland"">heartland</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;The central part of a <a href=""#region""><span title=""See entry on this page at § region"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">region</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;A part of a region considered essential to the viability and survival of the whole.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heath"" title=""Heath"">heath</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>heathland</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A shrubland habitat found mainly on free-draining, infertile, acidic soils and characterized by open, low-growing, woody vegetation.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hedgerow"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hedgerow"">hedgerow</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also simply called a <b>hedge</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A line of closely spaced shrubs or trees, planted and trained so as to form a barrier, to mark the boundary between two neighboring areas, or to serve as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Windbreak"" title=""Windbreak"">windbreak</a> for crops in adjacent fields.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemispheres_of_the_Earth"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hemispheres of the Earth"">hemisphere</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Half of the Earth, usually conceived as resulting from the division of the globe into two equal parts of either <a href=""#northern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Northern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">north</span></a> and <a href=""#southern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Southern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">south</span></a> or <a href=""#eastern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Eastern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">east</span></a> and <a href=""#western_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Western Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">west</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-11"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland"" title=""Highland"">highland</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Sometimes used interchangeably with <b><a href=""#upland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § upland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">upland</span></a></b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;Any elevated region of land, often one that is <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountainous</span></a> or situated atop a <a href=""#plateau""><span title=""See entry on this page at § plateau"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">plateau</span></a>. The term is sometimes reserved for relatively low-elevation <a href=""#mountain_range""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain range"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain ranges</span></a> or <a href=""#foothills""><span title=""See entry on this page at § foothills"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">foothills</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;Any area of land (mountainous or otherwise) that is higher in <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> relative to another area. In this sense, the term is often used as a conditional descriptor to distinguish related <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitat"" title=""Habitat"">habitats</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem"" title=""Ecosystem"">ecosystems</a>, especially freshwater <a href=""#riparian_area""><span title=""See entry on this page at § riparian area"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">riparian areas</span></a>, on the basis of elevation above sea level.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highway"" title=""Highway"">highway</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any major public or private road or other thoroughfare on land, especially one that is paved and capable of supporting high-capacity, rapid transit between populated places.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hill"" title=""Hill"">hill</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any landform that extends above the surrounding <a href=""#terrain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § terrain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">terrain</span></a>. A hill is generally considered less steep than a <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillock"" title=""Hillock"">hillock</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>knoll</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A small <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinterland"" title=""Hinterland"">hinterland</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An area that is tributary to a place and linked to that place through lines of exchange or interaction.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-61"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_geography"" title=""Historical geography"">historical geography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of <a href=""#human_geography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § human geography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">human geography</span></a> that studies the ways in which geographic phenomena have changed over time, especially (though not necessarily limited to) geographic change as it relates to human activity.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hogback_(geology)"" title=""Hogback (geology)"">hogback</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizon"" title=""Horizon"">horizon</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>skyline</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The apparent line that separates the ground from the sky, dividing all visible directions into two categories: those that intersect the Earth's surface and those that do not. When not obscured by buildings, trees, or mountains, the true horizon can be useful in navigation and determining positional orientation.</dd>
"
"horn"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a> formed by the back-to-back abutment of three or four adjacent <a href=""#cirque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cirque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cirques</span></a>, leaving a distinctly pyramidal peak.<sup id=""cite_ref-Mayhew_4-1"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Mayhew-4"">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horst_(geology)"" title=""Horst (geology)"">horst</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A raised block of the Earth's <a href=""#crust""><span title=""See entry on this page at § crust"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">crust</span></a>, bounded by parallel <a href=""#escarpment""><span title=""See entry on this page at § escarpment"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">escarpments</span></a> or <a href=""#fault""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fault"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">faults</span></a>, that has been displaced upward or has remained stationary while adjacent blocks on either side, known as <a href=""#graben""><span title=""See entry on this page at § graben"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">grabens</span></a>, have been displaced downward. Horsts and grabens often occur side-by-side in a repeated series of vertical displacements.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)"" title=""Hotspot (geology)"">hotspot</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An area in the middle of a <a href=""#lithospheric_plate""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lithospheric plate"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lithospheric plate</span></a> where <a href=""#magma""><span title=""See entry on this page at § magma"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">magma</span></a> rises from the mantle and erupts at the Earth's surface. <a href=""#volcano""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volcano"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Volcanoes</span></a> sometimes occur above a hotspot.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-15"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_geography"" title=""Human geography"">human geography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of geography that studies humans and their communities, cultures, economies, and interactions with the environment by examining their relations with and across space and place. Along with <a href=""#physical_geography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § physical geography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">physical geography</span></a>, it is one of the two major sub-fields of geography.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hummock"" title=""Hummock"">hummock</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A small <a href=""#knoll""><span title=""See entry on this page at § knoll"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">knoll</span></a> or <a href=""#mound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mound</span></a>, typically less than 15 metres (49&#160;ft) in height and situated above an otherwise level ground surface.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humus"" title=""Humus"">humus</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Partially decomposed organic soil material.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-62"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrography"" title=""Hydrography"">hydrography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The study of the surface waters of the Earth.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-63"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrosphere"" title=""Hydrosphere"">hydrosphere</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The totality of the water found on, under, and above the Earth's surface in liquid, solid, and gaseous forms, including all <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">oceans</span></a>, <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lakes</span></a>, <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">rivers</span></a>, and <a href=""#stream""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stream"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">streams</span></a>, as well as all ice and <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glaciers</span></a> and subsurface <a href=""#groundwater""><span title=""See entry on this page at § groundwater"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">groundwater</span></a>. Some definitions restrict the hydrosphere to liquid water only, instead placing solid forms in the <a href=""#cryosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cryosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cryosphere</span></a> and gaseous forms in the <a href=""#atmosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atmosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atmosphere</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-16"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypsography"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hypsography"">hypsography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The geographic representation on a <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a> of features related to <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a>, <a href=""#altitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § altitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">altitude</span></a>, and other measures of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Height"" title=""Height"">height</a> above a reference surface.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypsometry"" title=""Hypsometry"">hypsometry</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The study or measurement of land <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> relative to mean <a href=""#sea_level""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sea level"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sea level</span></a>. Hypsometry is the terrestrial equivalent of <a href=""#bathymetry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bathymetry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bathymetry</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_age"" title=""Ice age"">ice age</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A time of widespread <a href=""#glaciation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glaciation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glaciation</span></a>, such as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleistocene_Epoch"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Pleistocene Epoch"">Pleistocene Epoch</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-64"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_cap"" title=""Ice cap"">ice cap</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A flattened, often dome-shaped mass of ice that covers less than 50,000 square kilometres (19,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land area and is not constrained by topographical features such as mountains; larger masses of ice are termed <a href=""#ice_sheet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ice sheet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ice sheets</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""#polar_ice_cap""><span title=""See entry on this page at § polar ice cap"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">polar ice cap</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_sheet"" title=""Ice sheet"">ice sheet</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>continental glacier</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A mass of <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacial</span></a> ice that covers more than 50,000 square kilometres (19,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land area; smaller masses of ice may be termed <a href=""#ice_cap""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ice cap"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ice caps</span></a> or <a href=""#ice_shelf""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ice shelf"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ice shelves</span></a>. The two <a href=""#polar_ice_cap""><span title=""See entry on this page at § polar ice cap"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">polar ice sheets</span></a> are the only ice sheets that currently exist on Earth.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_shelf"" title=""Ice shelf"">ice shelf</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
