"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rural"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Rural"">rural</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An adjective describing any geographic area located outside areas of significant human population such as <a href=""#town""><span title=""See entry on this page at § town"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">towns</span></a> and <a href=""#city""><span title=""See entry on this page at § city"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cities</span></a>; all population, housing, and territory not included within an <a href=""#urban""><span title=""See entry on this page at § urban"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">urban</span></a> area is often said to be rural. Rural areas are typified by low <a href=""#population_density""><span title=""See entry on this page at § population density"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">population densities</span></a>, very small <a href=""#settlement""><span title=""See entry on this page at § settlement"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">settlements</span></a>, and expansive areas of agricultural land or <a href=""#wilderness""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wilderness"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wilderness</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddle_(landform)"" title=""Saddle (landform)"">saddle</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
<p>Maybe consider making a contribution to this <a href=""https://github.com/darigovresearch/Wikipedia-Glossary-of-Geography-Terms-Flashcardsroject</a> or even better improve the original Wikipedia article <a href=""httpshttps://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_geography_termsGlossary of physicsgeorgraphy termsa> by aking a contribution and <a href=""https://twitter.com/darigovresearch"">let us know</a>!</p>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salient_(geography)"" title=""Salient (geography)"">salient</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>panhandle</b>, <b>chimney</b> (if protruding northward), or <b>bootheel</b> (if protruding southward).</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any narrow, elongated protrusion of a larger <a href=""#territory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § territory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">territory</span></a>, either physical or political, such as a state.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-109"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_pan_(geology)"" title=""Salt pan (geology)"">salt pan</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>salt flat</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A large, flat expanse of land naturally covered with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt"" title=""Salt"">salt</a> and/or other minerals, usually to the exclusion of virtually all vegetation. Salt pans are common in <a href=""#desert""><span title=""See entry on this page at § desert"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">deserts</span></a>, where they form by the precipitation of dissolved mineral solids as a large body of water evaporates.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seawater"" title=""Seawater"">salt water</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>seawater</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any naturally occurring water, especially the water from a <a href=""#sea""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sea"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sea</span></a> or <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a>, characterized by high concentrations (between 3 and 5% by volume) of dissolved salts, primarily <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium"" title=""Sodium"">sodium</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloride"" title=""Chloride"">chloride</a> ions, relative to <a href=""#fresh_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fresh water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">fresh water</span></a>. Salt water in the Earth's oceans has an average salinity of about 3.5%; it is both denser and freezes at a lower temperature than fresh water.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scale_(map)"" title=""Scale (map)"">scale</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The relationship between a linear measurement on a <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a> and the distance it represents on the Earth's surface.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-19"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Escarpment"" title=""Escarpment"">scarp</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called an <b><a href=""#escarpment""><span title=""See entry on this page at § escarpment"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">escarpment</span></a></b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A steep <a href=""#cliff""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cliff"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cliff</span></a> or steep slope, formed either because of faulting or by the erosion of inclined rock strata.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-110"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea"" title=""Sea"">sea</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;Any large body of <a href=""#salt_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § salt water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">salt water</span></a> surrounded in whole or in part by land.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;Any large subdivision of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Ocean"" title=""World Ocean"">World Ocean</a>. ""The sea"" is the colloquial term for the entire interconnected system of salty bodies of water, including <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">oceans</span></a>, that covers the Earth.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_level"" title=""Sea level"">sea level</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The average level of the surface of one or more of Earth's <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">oceans</span></a> from which heights such as <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> and <a href=""#altitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § altitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">altitude</span></a> are commonly measured. Often called <i>mean sea level</i> (MSL), it is a type of standardized <a href=""#geodesy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodesy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">geodetic</span></a> <a href=""#geodetic_datum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geodetic datum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vertical datum</span></a> that is used in numerous applications, including <a href=""#surveying""><span title=""See entry on this page at § surveying"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">surveying</span></a>, <a href=""#cartography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cartography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cartography</span></a>, and <a href=""#navigation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § navigation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">navigation</span></a>. Mean sea level is commonly defined as the midpoint between the mean low and mean high <a href=""#tide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tides</span></a> at a particular location.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-20"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seabed"" title=""Seabed"">seabed</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seamount"" title=""Seamount"">seamount</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a> (often a <a href=""#volcano""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volcano"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volcano</span></a>) rising from the <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean_floor"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Ocean floor"">floor</a> whose <a href=""#summit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § summit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">summit</span></a> does not reach the water's surface and which is therefore entirely submerged and not an <a href=""#island""><span title=""See entry on this page at § island"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">island</span></a> or <a href=""#islet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § islet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">islet</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacation_property"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Vacation property"">second home</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A seasonally occupied dwelling that is not the primary residence of the owner. Such residences are usually found in areas with substantial opportunities for recreation or tourist activity.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-111"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"secondary-intercardinal directions"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The set of eight intermediate directions used in <a href=""#cartography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cartography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cartography</span></a> and <a href=""#navigation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § navigation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">navigation</span></a>, each of which is located halfway between a pair of <a href=""#intercardinal_directions""><span title=""See entry on this page at § intercardinal directions"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">intercardinal directions</span></a>: north-northeast (NNE), east-northeast (ENE), east-southeast (ESE), south-southeast (SSE), south-southwest (SSW), west-southwest (WSW), west-northwest (WNW), and north-northwest (NNW). They may or may not be explicitly labeled on a <a href=""#compass_rose""><span title=""See entry on this page at § compass rose"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">compass rose</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_sector"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Secondary sector"">secondary sector</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">That portion of a region's economy devoted to the processing of basic materials extracted by the <a href=""#primary_sector""><span title=""See entry on this page at § primary sector"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">primary sector</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-112"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"sector principle"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The principle on which political claims to territory in the <a href=""#polar_region""><span title=""See entry on this page at § polar region"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">polar regions</span></a> have historically been made, such that the territories are divided into arbitrary wedge-shaped sectors, each one having an apex at the geographic pole and including outer areas of both land and sea extending to a particular <a href=""#latitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § latitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">latitude</span></a>. Because of the limited accessibility and generally low material value of both the <a href=""#arctic""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Arctic"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Arctic</span></a> and <a href=""#antarctic""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Antarctic"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Antarctic</span></a>, the sector principle has emerged as a means of formally sharing responsibility for these regions between the world's sovereign states.<sup id=""cite_ref-Mayhew_4-6"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Mayhew-4"">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sedimentary_rock"" title=""Sedimentary rock"">sedimentary rock</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Rock formed by the hardening of material deposited in some process; most commonly sandstone, shale, and limestone.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-113"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seismograph"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Seismograph"">seismograph</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A scientific instrument that detects and records vibrations (seismic waves) produced by <a href=""#earthquake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § earthquake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">earthquakes</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-28"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_settlement"" title=""Human settlement"">settlement</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>locality</b> or <b><a href=""#populated_place""><span title=""See entry on this page at § populated place"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">populated place</span></a></b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any place where people live and form communities.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheepback"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Sheepback"">sheepback</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>rôche moutonnée</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A rock formation created by the passage of a <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacier</span></a> over underlying <a href=""#bedrock""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bedrock"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bedrock</span></a>, which often results in asymmetrical erosional forms created by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrasion_(geology)"" title=""Abrasion (geology)"">abrasion</a> on the upstream side of the rock and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plucking_(glaciation)"" title=""Plucking (glaciation)"">plucking</a> on the downstream side.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shield_(geology)"" title=""Shield (geology)"">shield</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A broad area of very old rocks above sea level that is usually characterized by thin, poor soils and low population densities.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-114"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shield_volcano"" title=""Shield volcano"">shield volcano</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A volcano that resembles an inverted warrior's shield. It has long gentle slopes produced by multiple eruptions of fluid lava flows.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-29"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shoal"" title=""Shoal"">shoal</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>sandbank</b>, <b>sandbar</b>, or <b>gravel bar</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A natural submerged <a href=""#ridge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ridge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ridge</span></a>, <a href=""#bank""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bank"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bank</span></a>, or <a href=""#bar""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bar"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bar</span></a> that consists of or is covered by sand or other unconsolidated material and rises from the bed of a <a href=""#body_of_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § body of water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">body of water</span></a> to just below or above the surface.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shore"" title=""Shore"">shore</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>shoreline</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The fringe of land at the edge of a large <a href=""#body_of_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § body of water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">body of water</span></a>, such as an <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a>, <a href=""#sea""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sea"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sea</span></a>, or <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a>. Compare <i><a href=""#coast""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coast"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coast</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinkhole"" title=""Sinkhole"">sinkhole</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#crater""><span title=""See entry on this page at § crater"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">crater</span></a> formed when the roof of a <a href=""#cave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cavern</span></a> collapses, usually found in areas of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limestone"" title=""Limestone"">limestone</a> rock.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-115"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Location_(geography)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Location (geography)"">site</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The features of a place related to the immediate environment on which the place is located (e.g. <a href=""#terrain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § terrain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">terrain</span></a>, soil, subsurface, geology, groundwater).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-116"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"situation"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The features of a place related to its location relative to other places (e.g., accessibility, hinterland quality).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-117"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slough_(hydrology)"" title=""Slough (hydrology)"">slough</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#wetland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wetland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wetland</span></a> – usually a <a href=""#swamp""><span title=""See entry on this page at § swamp"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">swamp</span></a>, a shallow <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a>, or a <a href=""#backwater""><span title=""See entry on this page at § backwater"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">backwater</span></a> branching from or feeding into a <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a> – in which water tends to be stagnant or flows only very slowly on a seasonal basis.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smog"" title=""Smog"">smog</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A mixture of particulate matter and chemical pollutants in the lower atmosphere, usually over urban areas.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-118"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Standard Metropolitan Statistical Area"">Standard Metropolitan Statistical Area</a> (SMSA)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A statistical unit of one or more counties that focus on one or more central cities larger than a specified size, or with a total population larger than a specified size. This is a reflection of urbanization.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-119"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snowline"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Snowline"">snowline</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The lowest elevation at which snow remains from year to year and does not melt during the summer.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-30"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil_horizon"" title=""Soil horizon"">soil horizon</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A distinct layer of soil encountered in vertical section.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-120"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solubility"" title=""Solubility"">solubility</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The degree to which a substance can be dissolved in another substance; in a geographical context, the characteristic of soil minerals that leads them to be carried away in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solution"" title=""Solution"">solution</a> by water (see <i><a href=""#leaching""><span title=""See entry on this page at § leaching"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">leaching</span></a></i>).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-121"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sound_(geography)"" title=""Sound (geography)"">sound</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;A large <a href=""#inlet""><span title=""See entry on this page at § inlet"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">inlet</span></a> of a sea or ocean that is larger than a <a href=""#bay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bay</span></a>, deeper than a <a href=""#bight""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bight"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bight</span></a>, and wider than a <a href=""#fjord""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fjord"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">fjord</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;A narrow sea or ocean <a href=""#channel""><span title=""See entry on this page at § channel"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">channel</span></a> between two landmasses.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Pole"" title=""South Pole"">South Geographic Pole</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>Geographic South Pole</b>, <b>Geographic South</b>, or simply the <b>South Pole</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The point in the <a href=""#southern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Southern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Southern Hemisphere</span></a> where the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation"" title=""Earth&#39;s rotation"">Earth's axis of rotation</a> meets its surface. It is the southernmost point on Earth, directly opposite the <a href=""#north_Geographic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § North Geographic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">North Geographic Pole</span></a>, and is located on continental land in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica"" title=""Antarctica"">Antarctica</a> at a <a href=""#latitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § latitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">latitude</span></a> of 90 degrees South; its <a href=""#longitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longitude</span></a> can be assigned any degree value. See also <i><a href=""#south_Magnetic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § South Magnetic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">South Magnetic Pole</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomagnetic_poles"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Geomagnetic poles"">South Geomagnetic Pole</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>Geomagnetic South Pole</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The point in the <a href=""#southern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Southern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Southern Hemisphere</span></a> where the axis of a theoretical simplified <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dipole"" title=""Dipole"">dipole</a> passing through the center of the Earth would intersect the Earth's surface. It is <a href=""#antipodes""><span title=""See entry on this page at § antipodes"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">antipodal</span></a> to the <a href=""#north_Geomagnetic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § North Geomagnetic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">North Geomagnetic Pole</span></a>. Because of the fluid nature of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_core"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Earth&#39;s core"">Earth's molten core</a>, the true axis of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field"" title=""Earth&#39;s magnetic field"">Earth's magnetic field</a> is not a perfect dipole, and so the Geomagnetic Poles and the actual <a href=""#south_Magnetic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § South Magnetic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Magnetic Poles</span></a> lie some distance apart.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Magnetic_Pole"" title=""South Magnetic Pole"">South Magnetic Pole</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>Magnetic South Pole</b> or <b>Magnetic South</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The point in the <a href=""#southern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Southern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Southern Hemisphere</span></a> at which the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field"" title=""Earth&#39;s magnetic field"">Earth's magnetic field</a> points vertically downward. It is close to but distinct from the <a href=""#south_Geographic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § South Geographic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Geographic South Pole</span></a> and the <a href=""#south_Geomagnetic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § South Geomagnetic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Geomagnetic South Pole</span></a>, and its precise location varies considerably over time due to frequent magnetic changes in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_core"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Earth&#39;s core"">Earth's core</a>. Its counterpart in the Northern Hemisphere is the <a href=""#north_Magnetic_Pole""><span title=""See entry on this page at § North Magnetic Pole"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">North Magnetic Pole</span></a>, though the two poles are not directly opposite each other.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere"" title=""Southern Hemisphere"">Southern Hemisphere</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">half sphere</span></a> of the Earth that is south of the <a href=""#equator""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Equator"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Equator</span></a>. It is opposite the <a href=""#northern_Hemisphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Northern Hemisphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Northern Hemisphere</span></a>.</dd>
"
"space economy"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The locational pattern of economic activities and their interconnecting linkages.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-122"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spatial_citizenship"" title=""Spatial citizenship"">spatial citizenship</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The participation of individuals and groups of laypeople in decision-making about <a href=""#spatial_planning""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spatial planning"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spatial planning</span></a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convention_(norm)"" title=""Convention (norm)"">social rules</a> in public spaces through the reflexive production and use of geographic media such as <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">maps</span></a>, <a href=""#virtual_globe""><span title=""See entry on this page at § virtual globe"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">virtual globes</span></a>, and <a href=""#geographic_information_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geographic information system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">GIS</span></a> software, particularly in order to question existing perspectives on the appropriation of space and the actions permitted within that space and to negotiate alternative spatial visions.</dd>
"
"spatial complementarity"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The occurrence of location pairing such that items demanded by one place can be supplied by another.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-123"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"spatial interaction"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Movement between locationally separate places.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-124"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spatial_reference_system"" title=""Spatial reference system"">spatial reference system</a> (SRS)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>coordinate reference system (CRS)</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#geographic_coordinate_system""><span title=""See entry on this page at § geographic coordinate system"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coordinate-based</span></a> local, regional, or global system used to locate geographical entities and which defines a specific <a href=""#map_projection""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map projection"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map projection</span></a> as well as transformations between different systems.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spirit_level"" title=""Spirit level"">spirit level</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spit_(landform)"" title=""Spit (landform)"">spit</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>sandspit</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#bar""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bar"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bar</span></a> or <a href=""#shoal""><span title=""See entry on this page at § shoal"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">shoal</span></a> extending from a <a href=""#beach""><span title=""See entry on this page at § beach"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">beach</span></a> into an <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a> or <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a> and which develops by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deposition_(geology)"" title=""Deposition (geology)"">deposition</a> of sediment as a result of <a href=""#longshore_drift""><span title=""See entry on this page at § longshore drift"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">longshore drift</span></a>. Spits form where the direction of the <a href=""#shore""><span title=""See entry on this page at § shore"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">shoreline</span></a> sharply changes direction, such as at a <a href=""#headland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § headland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">headland</span></a>, and often develop a ""hooked"" or recurve shape at their distal ends.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge"" title=""Mid-ocean ridge"">spreading ridges</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Places on the ocean floor where <a href=""#lithosphere""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lithosphere"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lithospheric</span></a> plates separate and magma erupts. About 80 percent of the Earth's volcanic activity occurs on the ocean floor.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-31"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spring_(hydrology)"" title=""Spring (hydrology)"">spring</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any location where water naturally emerges from an underground <a href=""#aquifer""><span title=""See entry on this page at § aquifer"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">aquifer</span></a> to the Earth's surface.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spur_(topography)"" title=""Spur (topography)"">spur</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A lateral <a href=""#ridge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ridge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ridge</span></a> or other salient landform protruding from the side of a <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a>, <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a>, or the main crest of a <a href=""#ridge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ridge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ridge</span></a> and typically surrounded on at least three sides by steep hillsides.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stack_(geology)"" title=""Stack (geology)"">stack</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>sea stack</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#coast""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coast"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coastal</span></a> landform consisting of a steep and often vertical column or columns of rock above the surface of the sea and formed by erosion due to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coastal_erosion#wave_action"" title=""Coastal erosion"">wave action</a>.</dd>
"
"stand"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An area of vegetation dominated by a single species, e.g. a stand of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oak"" title=""Oak"">oak</a> trees.<sup id=""cite_ref-Mayhew_4-7"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Mayhew-4"">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_(polity)"" title=""State (polity)"">state</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A compulsory political organization with a centralized government that maintains a monopoly of the legitimate use of force within a certain geographical territory. See <i><a href=""#country""><span title=""See entry on this page at § country"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">country</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steppe"" title=""Steppe"">steppe</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""#ecoregion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ecoregion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ecoregion</span></a> characterized by expansive grassland <a href=""#plain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § plain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">plains</span></a> without trees apart from those near rivers and lakes.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-32"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strait"" title=""Strait"">strait</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#channel""><span title=""See entry on this page at § channel"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">channel</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strath"" title=""Strath"">strath</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A large river <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a>, typically wider and shallower than a <a href=""#glen""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glen"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glen</span></a>. The term is used primarily in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland"" title=""Scotland"">Scotland</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia"" title=""Australia"">Australia</a>, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada"" title=""Canada"">Canada</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratovolcano"" title=""Stratovolcano"">stratovolcano</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>composite volcano</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A steep-sided <a href=""#volcano""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volcano"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volcano</span></a> built by <a href=""#lava""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lava"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lava</span></a> flows and <a href=""#tephra""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tephra"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tephra</span></a> deposits.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-33"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stream"" title=""Stream"">stream</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A natural body of water in which <a href=""#surface_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § surface water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">surface water</span></a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Streamflow"" title=""Streamflow"">flows</a> between the <a href=""#bank""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bank"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">banks</span></a> of a <a href=""#channel""><span title=""See entry on this page at § channel"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">channel</span></a>. Long, large streams are usually called <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">rivers</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Streambed"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Streambed"">streambed</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>riverbed</b> or simply a <b>bed</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The bottom of the <a href=""#channel""><span title=""See entry on this page at § channel"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">channel</span></a> of a <a href=""#stream""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stream"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stream</span></a> or <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a>, usually covered with rocks, sand, or debris and totally devoid of terrestrial vegetation if the stream has flowed recently. The bed is generally considered the part of the channel up to the normal water line, whereas the <a href=""#bank""><span title=""See entry on this page at § bank"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bank</span></a> is the part above the water line.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subduction_zone"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Subduction zone"">subduction zone</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The place where two lithospheric plates come together, one riding over the other. Most volcanoes on land occur parallel to and inland from the boundary between the two plates.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-34"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suburban"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Suburban"">suburban</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An adjective describing a mixed-use or residential area existing either as part of an <a href=""#urban""><span title=""See entry on this page at § urban"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">urban</span></a> area or as a separate community within <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commuting"" title=""Commuting"">commuting</a> distance of a <a href=""#city""><span title=""See entry on this page at § city"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">city</span></a>; a place of this type is called a <i>suburb</i>. Suburbs are often defined by commuter infrastructures and have lower population densities than inner-city <a href=""#neighborhood""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neighborhood"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neighborhoods</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suburbanization"" title=""Suburbanization"">suburbanization</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The process by which a human <a href=""#population""><span title=""See entry on this page at § population"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">population</span></a> shifts from <a href=""#urban""><span title=""See entry on this page at § urban"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">urban</span></a> to <a href=""#suburban""><span title=""See entry on this page at § suburban"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">suburban</span></a> residency, or the gradual increase in the proportion of people choosing to live in suburban neighborhoods which act as satellite communities within commuting distance of larger, centralized urban areas. Suburbanization is inversely related to <a href=""#urbanization""><span title=""See entry on this page at § urbanization"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">urbanization</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summit"" title=""Summit"">summit</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>acme</b>, <b>apex</b>, <b>peak</b>, and <b>zenith</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A point on a surface that is higher in <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> than all points immediately adjacent to it. Mathematically, it is a local maximum in elevation. The highest point of a <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a> or <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a> is often referred to as the summit.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_water"" title=""Surface water"">surface water</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Water present on the surface of the Earth, such as in a <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a>, <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a>, <a href=""#wetland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wetland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wetland</span></a>, or <a href=""#ocean""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ocean"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ocean</span></a>, as opposed to <a href=""#groundwater""><span title=""See entry on this page at § groundwater"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subsurface water</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surveying"" title=""Surveying"">surveying</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The science, technique, and profession of determining the terrestrial or three-dimensional positions of points on the surface of the Earth and the distances and angles between them. These points are often used to draw <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">maps</span></a> and establish <a href=""#border""><span title=""See entry on this page at § border"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">boundaries</span></a> for property ownership, construction projects, and other purposes required by civil law.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swale_(landform)"" title=""Swale (landform)"">swale</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any shallow <a href=""#channel""><span title=""See entry on this page at § channel"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">channel</span></a> or trough with gently sloping sides, either natural or artificial. Man-made swales are often designed to manage <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_runoff"" title=""Surface runoff"">surface runoff</a> and increase rainwater <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)"" title=""Infiltration (hydrology)"">infiltration</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swamp"" title=""Swamp"">swamp</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A forested <a href=""#wetland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wetland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wetland</span></a>, often occurring along a large <a href=""#river""><span title=""See entry on this page at § river"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">river</span></a> or on the shores of a large <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrt"" title=""Syrt"">syrt</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#denudation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § denudation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">denudational</span></a> <a href=""#highland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § highland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">highland</span></a> or elevated <a href=""https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/flatlands"" class=""extiw"" title=""wikt:flatlands"">flatland</a> in Russia and Central Asia; a kind of <a href=""#dissected_plateau""><span title=""See entry on this page at § dissected plateau"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">dissected plateau</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taiga"" title=""Taiga"">taiga</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A moist subarctic coniferous <a href=""#forest""><span title=""See entry on this page at § forest"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forest</span></a> that begins where the <a href=""#tundra""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tundra"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tundra</span></a> ends and is dominated by spruces and firs.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-125"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tarn_(lake)"" title=""Tarn (lake)"">tarn</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>corrie loch</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A mountain <a href=""#lake""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lake"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lake</span></a> or pool of water formed in a <a href=""#cirque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cirque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cirque</span></a> excavated by a <a href=""#glacier""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacier"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacier</span></a>. A <a href=""#moraine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § moraine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">moraine</span></a> may form a natural <a href=""#dam""><span title=""See entry on this page at § dam"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">dam</span></a> below a tarn.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperature_inversion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Temperature inversion"">temperature inversion</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An increase in temperature with height above the Earth's surface, a reversal of the normal pattern.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-126"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tephra"" title=""Tephra"">tephra</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Solid material of all sizes explosively ejected from a <a href=""#volcano""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volcano"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volcano</span></a> into the atmosphere.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-35"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrain"" title=""Terrain"">terrain</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>topographical relief</b> or simply <b>relief</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The vertical and horizontal dimensions of a land surface, usually as expressed in terms of <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slope"" title=""Slope"">slope</a>, and orientation of geographical features.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territorial_waters"" title=""Territorial waters"">territorial waters</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;A concept of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_the_Sea"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Law of the Sea"">Law of the Sea</a> defined as a belt of <a href=""#coast""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coast"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coastal</span></a> waters extending at most 12 nautical miles (22&#160;km) from the designated <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baseline_(sea)"" title=""Baseline (sea)"">baseline</a> (usually defined as the mean low-water line) for a coastal <a href=""#state""><span title=""See entry on this page at § state"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">state</span></a> and regarded as the sovereign <a href=""#territory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § territory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">territory</span></a> of the state.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;Any area of water over which a state has legal jurisdiction, including <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internal_waters"" title=""Internal waters"">internal waters</a>, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone"" title=""Exclusive economic zone"">exclusive economic zone</a>, and potentially others.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territory_(country_subdivision)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Territory (country subdivision)"">territory</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A specific area or portion of the Earth's surface; similar to though distinct from a <a href=""#region""><span title=""See entry on this page at § region"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">region</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-127"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertiary_sector"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Tertiary sector"">tertiary sector</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">That portion of a region's economy devoted to service activities (e.g., retail and wholesale operations, transportation, insurance).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-128"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thalweg"" title=""Thalweg"">thalweg</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The line of lowest <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> within a <a href=""#valley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § valley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">valley</span></a> or <a href=""#watercourse""><span title=""See entry on this page at § watercourse"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">watercourse</span></a>. Thalwegs often acquire special significance in <a href=""#political_geography""><span title=""See entry on this page at § political geography"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">political geography</span></a> because disputed <a href=""#border""><span title=""See entry on this page at § border"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">borders</span></a> along rivers are often defined as the river's thalweg.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tide"" title=""Tide"">tide</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The periodic rise and fall of <a href=""#sea_level""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sea level"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sea levels</span></a> caused by the combined effects of the gravitational forces exerted by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon"" title=""Moon"">Moon</a> and the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun"" title=""Sun"">Sun</a> and the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation"" title=""Earth&#39;s rotation"">Earth's rotation</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tide_pool"" title=""Tide pool"">tide pool</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A shallow pool of <a href=""#salt_water""><span title=""See entry on this page at § salt water"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">seawater</span></a>, supplied regularly by incoming <a href=""#tide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tides</span></a>, that forms on a rocky <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intertidal_zone"" title=""Intertidal zone"">intertidal</a> <a href=""#shore""><span title=""See entry on this page at § shore"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">shore</span></a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tied_island"" title=""Tied island"">tied island</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>land-tied island</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""#island""><span title=""See entry on this page at § island"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">island</span></a> that is connected to a <a href=""#mainland""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mainland"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mainland</span></a> only by a narrow <a href=""#spit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spit</span></a> or <a href=""#tombolo""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tombolo"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tombolo</span></a> which may or may not be occasionally submerged.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_till"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Glacial till"">till</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#glacial_till""><span title=""See entry on this page at § glacial till"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">glacial till</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time_distance"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Time distance"">time distance</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A time measure of how far apart places are (how long does it take to travel from place A to place B?). This may be contrasted with other distance metrics such as geographic distance (how far is it?) and cost-distance (how much will it cost to get there?).<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-129"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time_geography"" title=""Time geography"">time geography</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>time-space geography</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">An interdisciplinary perspective, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontology"" title=""Ontology"">ontological</a> framework, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visual_language"" title=""Visual language"">visual language</a> in which space and time are used as basic dimensions of analysis of dynamic processes and events, including social and ecological interactions, environmental changes, and biographies of individuals.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time_zone"" title=""Time zone"">time zone</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A region of the globe that observes a uniform <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_time"" title=""Standard time"">standard time</a> for legal, commercial, and social purposes.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tombolo"" title=""Tombolo"">tombolo</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topographic_map"" title=""Topographic map"">topographic map</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>relief map</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#map""><span title=""See entry on this page at § map"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">map</span></a> that uses <a href=""#contour_lines""><span title=""See entry on this page at § contour lines"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">contour lines</span></a> to represent the three-dimensional features of a landscape on a two-dimensional surface.<sup id=""cite_ref-USGS_2010_3-36"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-USGS_2010-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topographical_relief"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Topographical relief"">topographical relief</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#terrain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § terrain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">terrain</span></a></i>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topographic_isolation"" title=""Topographic isolation"">topographic isolation</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The minimum <a href=""#great-circle_distance""><span title=""See entry on this page at § great-circle distance"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">great-circle distance</span></a> between the <a href=""#summit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § summit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">summit</span></a> of a <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a> or <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a> and a point of equal <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a>, representing a radius of dominance in which the summit is the highest point.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topographic_prominence"" title=""Topographic prominence"">topographic prominence</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>autonomous height</b>, <b>relative height</b>, or <b>shoulder drop</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A measure of the independence of a <a href=""#mountain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mountain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mountain</span></a> or <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a> defined as the vertical distance between its <a href=""#summit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § summit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">summit</span></a> and the lowest <a href=""#contour_lines""><span title=""See entry on this page at § contour lines"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">contour line</span></a> encircling it but containing no higher summit within it or, equivalently, the difference between the <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevation</span></a> of the summit and the elevation of the <a href=""#key_col""><span title=""See entry on this page at § key col"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">key col</span></a>. Mountains with high prominence tend to be the highest points in their vicinity.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topography"" title=""Topography"">topography</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The physical features of a place, or the study and depiction of physical features, including <a href=""#terrain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § terrain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">terrain relief</span></a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-OERI_1996_1-21"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-OERI_1996-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toponymy"" title=""Toponymy"">toponymy</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The study of placenames (known as <i>toponyms</i>), their origins, meanings, use, and typology.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tor_(rock_formation)"" title=""Tor (rock formation)"">tor</a>"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>castle koppie</b> or <b>kopje</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A prominent, free-standing rock <a href=""#outcrop""><span title=""See entry on this page at § outcrop"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">outcrop</span></a> that rises abruptly from the smooth slopes of a gently rounded <a href=""#hill""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hill"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hill</span></a> or <a href=""#ridge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ridge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ridge</span></a>. In the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom"" title=""United Kingdom"">United Kingdom</a>, the term is also used to refer to the hill itself.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Town"" title=""Town"">town</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A medium-sized human <a href=""#settlement""><span title=""See entry on this page at § settlement"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">settlement</span></a> that is generally larger than a <a href=""#village""><span title=""See entry on this page at § village"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">village</span></a> but smaller than a <a href=""#city""><span title=""See entry on this page at § city"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">city</span></a>, though the criteria for distinguishing a town vary considerably in different parts of the world.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Township_and_range"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Township and range"">township and range</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The rectangular system of land subdivision of much of the agriculturally settled <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States"" title=""United States"">United States</a> west of the Appalachian Mountains, established by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Land_Ordinance_of_1785"" title=""Land Ordinance of 1785"">Land Ordinance of 1785</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-130"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"transferability"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The extent to which a good or service can be moved from one location to another; the relative capacity for spatial interaction.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-131"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transhumance"" title=""Transhumance"">transhumance</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The seasonal movement of people and animals in search of pasture. Commonly, winters are spent in snow-free lowlands and summers in the cooler uplands.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-132"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_line"" title=""Tree line"">tree line</a>"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#latitude""><span title=""See entry on this page at § latitude"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">latitudinal</span></a> or <a href=""#elevation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elevation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elevational</span></a> limit of normal tree growth. Beyond this limit (i.e. closer to the <a href=""#polar_region""><span title=""See entry on this page at § polar region"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">poles</span></a> or at higher elevations) climatic conditions are too severe for such growth and trees are stunted or entirely absent.<sup id=""cite_ref-IIP_2008_2-133"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-IIP_2008-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangulation_(surveying)"" title=""Triangulation (surveying)"">triangulation</a>"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>
